<div id="review_2011469513" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2011469513" />
    <a title="Mit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42022278-mit"><img alt="Mit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522148591p2/42022278.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2011469513?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mit" href="/user/show/42022278-mit">Mit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2011469513" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7078437530160450698"><i>"How men fear the chaos of the world, I thought, and the yawning eternity hereafter. So we build patterns to explain its terrible mysteries and reassure ourselves we are safe in this world and beyond."</i><br/><br/>First of all, this started promisingly. I was so much engrossed in the world of 16th century England. The thing was lively and really set the tone. But, the thing went downhill afterwards. When I picked up this book, I had hoped for a clever and a complex mystery. It did seem like one in the initia</span>
  <span id="freeText7078437530160450698" style="display:none"><i>"How men fear the chaos of the world, I thought, and the yawning eternity hereafter. So we build patterns to explain its terrible mysteries and reassure ourselves we are safe in this world and beyond."</i><br /><br />First of all, this started promisingly. I was so much engrossed in the world of 16th century England. The thing was lively and really set the tone. But, the thing went downhill afterwards. When I picked up this book, I had hoped for a clever and a complex mystery. It did seem like one in the initial pages. The main character, <b>Matthew Shardlake</b> alongside with his assistant <b>Mark Poer</b> goes to a monastery at Scarnsea to investigate the murder of a commissioner. The monastery is a strange place and gives negative and evil vibes and it really makes me feel like that some complex character with complex ideologies might be the culprit behind this. <br /><br />As the days goes by, we see back to back murders and Shardlake discovers a mess which he really did not expect. His character impressed me somehow. I found him as a very capable lawyer and also a very capable sleuth. However, at some point he is just not shown well. At one point I felt like he would not solve this without luck. I expected much more from other characters. I expected very much from Mark but it felt like he was just so much naive about so many things. Associating him with a <b>Alice</b>, a servant at the monastery was not such a good idea and it was the reason why this book did not impress me. <b>Both characters of Mark and Alice were turned out to be totally senseless.</b> The other characters like Jerome, Abbot Fabian, Brother Guy, Brother Gabriel were average. The intent behind murders were nothing but pure revenge and greed and that disappointed me a bit since I was expecting a very complex motive. I also think that this book just dragged on more than necessary. At times, we see Shardlake wasting time behind running from one end of monastery to another and questioning everyone which became very repetitive and boring. <br /><br />Despite all this shortcomings, I really liked the setup and historical link to this novel were accurate. i would recommend this to anyone who is into reading historical fiction. A casual reader of historical fiction will feel bored by some of the details in this book. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7078437530160450698" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14511926234301859581">'The Bible says God made man in his image but I think we make and remake him, in whatever image happens to suit our shifting needs.'<br/><br/>This is a complex time in Tudor history, brilliantly brought to life by CJ Sansom. The status quo in Britain at the time this story is told is an uneasy one. Thomas Cromwell has been commissioned by Henry VIII to dissolve the monasteries. Papists are hunted down.   <span id="freeText14511926234301859581" style="display:none">'The Bible says God made man in his image but I think we make and remake him, in whatever image happens to suit our shifting needs.'<br /><br />This is a complex time in Tudor history, brilliantly brought to life by CJ Sansom. The status quo in Britain at the time this story is told is an uneasy one. Thomas Cromwell has been commissioned by Henry VIII to dissolve the monasteries. Papists are hunted down. As one character says' there is nowhere safe in the world, no thing certain.'<br />Matthew Shardlake , a hunchbacked lawyer from London is charged with the investigation of one of the Kings commissioner's murder while auditing a monastery. He is a good man and well developed intelligent character. He knows he is not a 'catch' for any woman because of his disability and yet it is painful to read of his hopes of genuine feelings between himself and a serving girl, and also to see his disillusionment with Reformers' dishonesty and corruption.<br />I loved this book more when I was more of a historical fictionista but since I have been seduced by historical fantasy, I am slightly less keen. <br />Still a really enjoyable and quality book and I will probably over time reread the others in the series. It seems a long time since the last volume by CJ Sansom and I think my urge to re read this came from my longing for a continuation of Matthew Shardlakes adventures.</span>

<div id="review_2524142541" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2524142541" />
    <a title="Lady Clementina ffinch-ffarowmore" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore"><img alt="Lady Clementina ffinch-ffarowmore" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560079929p2/5917681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2524142541?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lady Clementina ffinch-ffarowmore" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lady Clementina ffinch-ffarowmore" href="/user/show/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore">Lady Clementina ffinch-ffarowmore</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=matthew-shardlake">matthew-shardlake</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=murder">murder</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=tudor">tudor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2524142541" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13867723856942193299">This is the first book* in the excellent Matthew Shardlake series by Sansom, which I am reading slightly out of order, having started with book 2 (which I really enjoyed). In this, we are first introduced to Shardlake, a confidant of Cromwell, who is given a commission by the latter to investigate a murder committed at a monastery in Scarnsea. It is 1537, Henry VIII has declared himself head of the church of England and the process of dissolving all the monasteries in the country, now the larger</span>
  <span id="freeText13867723856942193299" style="display:none">This is the first book* in the excellent Matthew Shardlake series by Sansom, which I am reading slightly out of order, having started with book 2 (which I really enjoyed). In this, we are first introduced to Shardlake, a confidant of Cromwell, who is given a commission by the latter to investigate a murder committed at a monastery in Scarnsea. It is 1537, Henry VIII has declared himself head of the church of England and the process of dissolving all the monasteries in the country, now the larger and wealthier ones (after some smaller ones earlier on) is on, and Cromwell has appointed commissioners to see to it that this is accomplished. When Cromwell’s commissioner to Scarnsea Robert Singleton is brutally murdered, Shardlake charged with finding the murderer heads there with his assistant Mark Poer, only to find that things are very very murky in the monastery though on the surface, everything seems fine. The matter is further complicated when other deaths occur. Not only that, the people he encounters and the stories he hears begin to make him question all he that has believed so far.<br /><br />Shardlake’s first outing was not perhaps as complicated in terms of the mystery threads in the story as the later ones, which is not to say by any means that it was an easy case—there were many threads to this as well, and the answers when they are revealed are not quite what one expected (incidentally, I did successfully guess one part of it, so the surprise element was definitely there). The atmosphere of the country at that point in time, the uncertainty, the turmoil, with the shape of religion changing alongside the changing scope of power, is something that the author I felt has captured really well, as he does the details of everyday life in Tudor England. Shardlake himself, I felt from the beginning came across as too idealistic and naïve compared to the later books, and while Mark Poer was an interesting enough character, “seeing” things much more clearly in some ways than Shardlake, having become used to Jack Barrack from the later titles, somehow, one doesn’t get the same kind of feeling about him. I enjoyed seeing how Shardlake meets Gut of Molton, though. This was a well written and very enjoyable entry in the series, though I probably think I would have enjoyed it far more had I read it before the later ones. <br /><br />* The seventh book in the series will be out in about a month, and I am very much looking forward to reading that.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13867723856942193299" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_201727338" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/201727338" />
    <a title="Michael Cattigan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4539230-michael-cattigan"><img alt="Michael Cattigan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596819263p2/4539230.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/201727338?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Cattigan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Cattigan" href="/user/show/4539230-michael-cattigan">Michael Cattigan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer201727338" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4596344903406788660">Looking at the reviews here, it seems that this book is getting hammered because it cries out to be compared with other powerhouses of books. <br/><br/>Set in the 1500s of Henry VIII, it clearly bears parallels with Wolf Hall which is set two wives earlier. It has to be said that it lacks the beauty of the language of that novel or its subtle, multilayered realistic characterisation. Mantell's Cromwell is a far more engaging and convincing narrator than Sansom's Shardlake.<br/><br/>Similarly, set in an isolated mon</span>
  <span id="freeText4596344903406788660" style="display:none">Looking at the reviews here, it seems that this book is getting hammered because it cries out to be compared with other powerhouses of books. <br /><br />Set in the 1500s of Henry VIII, it clearly bears parallels with Wolf Hall which is set two wives earlier. It has to be said that it lacks the beauty of the language of that novel or its subtle, multilayered realistic characterisation. Mantell's Cromwell is a far more engaging and convincing narrator than Sansom's Shardlake.<br /><br />Similarly, set in an isolated monastery, narrated by an articulate first person narrator who is investigating a murder, comparisons with Name of the Rose are easy to make. I may be mistaken, but I wonder whether the reference to the (fake) lost Comedy of Aristotle in Scarnsea's library was a deliberate echo (or Eco?) of Name of the Rose.Again, however, Dissolution comes off worse in the comparison: it lacks the intensely almost arcanely intellectualism of Eco, who I love hugely; the language comes across as being broadly modern with the occasional nod towards the period whereas Eco's writing has always struck me an amazingly authentic.<br /><br />This said, look at the writers with whom Sansom is being compared: I cannot think of any writer who would come off better in such a contest! It is the fact of the parallels that almost demand the comparisons to be made which is the problem, not the writing or the plotting itself. Sansom has so far succeeded in engaging and entertaining me, creating a universe which feels reasonably authentic. That seems to me to be worth a good 4 stars... </span>
  <a data-text-id="4596344903406788660" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_947643236" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/947643236" />
    <a title="Samantha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5880552-samantha"><img alt="Samantha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493644616p2/5880552.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/947643236?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Samantha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Samantha" href="/user/show/5880552-samantha">Samantha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5880552-samantha?shelf=mysterious">mysterious</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5880552-samantha?shelf=own-it">own-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5880552-samantha?shelf=more-historical-than-fiction">more-historical-than-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5880552-samantha?shelf=tudors">tudors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5880552-samantha?shelf=british-history">british-history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer947643236" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9280161277744440417">Set during the Dissolution of the Monasteries under Henry VIII, this novel brings this episode in history to life through the character Matthew Shardlake. He is developed throughout the story, creating a multi-faceted, compelling protagonist. Passers by only see Shardlake as a cripple, but the reader sees his pride, insecurities, longing for companionship, and devotion to a cause that he believes is sincere.<br/><br/>Through the example of the Monastery of St. Donatus at Scarnsea, we are shown first hand </span>
  <span id="freeText9280161277744440417" style="display:none">Set during the Dissolution of the Monasteries under Henry VIII, this novel brings this episode in history to life through the character Matthew Shardlake. He is developed throughout the story, creating a multi-faceted, compelling protagonist. Passers by only see Shardlake as a cripple, but the reader sees his pride, insecurities, longing for companionship, and devotion to a cause that he believes is sincere.<br /><br />Through the example of the Monastery of St. Donatus at Scarnsea, we are shown first hand what it was like for the monks at this time to be at the mercy of the king. First they were ordered to change centuries old rituals to fit the reformist trend, only to eventually lose their entire way of life. Putting it on this personal level and seeing what happens to each of the characters Sansom creates, the reader can vividly imagine this period of history.<br /><br />The author's depiction of Tudor times is second to none. Daily living and all its struggles are injected into this story without becoming info-dumps or narrations of boring detail. Cromwell makes a couple of brief appearances, and he is realistically written, if not as sympathetically as in Mantel's novels.<br /><br />I appreciated the author's ability to give each of his characters an active faith that did not truly fit into any category that the government of the time was attempting to create. Shardlake proclaims himself an ardent reformer, until he is struck with the realization that the reforms are more about redistribution of wealth than true worship. Though the setting was a religious house and each person revealed their thoughts on God, the book never overdoes the Christian theme or becomes preachy.<br /><br />And then there are the murders! I loved this book for the great character development and visualization of Tudor England, but it is also a great mystery. Shardlake is sent to Scarnsea to investigate the murder of his fellow commissioner and discovers a much more tangled web than he expected. <br /><br />A must read for anyone interested in this period of history.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9280161277744440417" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Diane Barnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7262060-diane-barnes"><img alt="Diane Barnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329773692p2/7262060.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1176326362?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane Barnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane Barnes" href="/user/show/7262060-diane-barnes">Diane Barnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7262060-diane-barnes?shelf=noble-bitches-book-club">noble-bitches-book-club</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1176326362" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11283006426789499580">This is a great murder mystery set in the 16th century, during the religious reformation of Thomas Cromwell and Henry VIII. The setting is a monastery in Scarnsea, and Matthew Shardlake is a hunchbacked lawyer who serves as a commissioner of Cromwell to investigate the murder, and to hasten the dissolving of St. Donatus, as a precedent and warning to other religious orders as to what awaits them. One murder turns into 4, with twists and turns and red herrings all along the way. This book has it </span>
  <span id="freeText11283006426789499580" style="display:none">This is a great murder mystery set in the 16th century, during the religious reformation of Thomas Cromwell and Henry VIII. The setting is a monastery in Scarnsea, and Matthew Shardlake is a hunchbacked lawyer who serves as a commissioner of Cromwell to investigate the murder, and to hasten the dissolving of St. Donatus, as a precedent and warning to other religious orders as to what awaits them. One murder turns into 4, with twists and turns and red herrings all along the way. This book has it all: excellent writing, great plot, flawed, believable characters, and it even throws in discussion of organized religion and who it actually serves, as well. Add to that a well researched time period that I became interested in with Hilary Mantel's "Wolf Hall" and "Bring up the Bodies", and I'm hooked. Fortunately, there are more in this series, and I'll be adding them to my already unwieldy tbr shelves. Highly recommended to any mystery lover.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11283006426789499580" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2395362956" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2395362956" />
    <a title="Susan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5837456-susan"><img alt="Susan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1372225128p2/5837456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2395362956?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan" href="/user/show/5837456-susan">Susan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/13537979-dissolution">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2395362956" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6740409998467129013">First published in 2003, this is the first novel in the Matthew Shardlake series, and introduces us to our unlikely hero; lovelorn, hunchbacked, a reformist lawyer who begins the book as utterly loyal to Cromwell’s ideals and ends it plagued with doubts about his role and mission. <br/><br/>Shardlake is sent by Cromwell to the Monastery of St Donatus the Ascendant at Scarnsea, Sussex. It is 1537 and the dissolution of the monasteries is underway. Cromwell had sent Commissioner Robin Singleton there with l</span>
  <span id="freeText6740409998467129013" style="display:none">First published in 2003, this is the first novel in the Matthew Shardlake series, and introduces us to our unlikely hero; lovelorn, hunchbacked, a reformist lawyer who begins the book as utterly loyal to Cromwell’s ideals and ends it plagued with doubts about his role and mission. <br /><br />Shardlake is sent by Cromwell to the Monastery of St Donatus the Ascendant at Scarnsea, Sussex. It is 1537 and the dissolution of the monasteries is underway. Cromwell had sent Commissioner Robin Singleton there with lawyer, Lawrence Goodhaps. Now Singleton has been found murdered and Shardlake is sent to investigate. He is accompanied by Mark Poer, a young man he had taken into his household as a family obligation. Mark has been in disgrace for his dalliance with a knight’s daughter and Shardlake hopes that success in their mission will plead Poer’s case for rehabilitation, while putting him in Cromwell’s favour. However, once they arrive they find Goodhaps terrified and the monks suspicious and concerned about their future. Snow begins to fall, making travel difficult, and the monastery is isolated, with dangerous marshland outside the gates and the possibility of smugglers. Before long, Shardlake is embroiled in an investigation of murder, which reaches back to the trial and beheading of Ann Boleyn – an event which Shardlake attended on Cromwell’s orders. <br /><br />Although this is the first novel in the series, and there are bumps along the way, the character of Shardlake himself already seems like an old friend. The setting – the isolated monastery with a fixed group of suspects– works very well. Mark Poer is not the greatest foil to Shardlake, but the novel introduces other characters who appear in later books. Overall, even re-reading this, it is an assured and excellent debut to a series which has to be my favourite historical mystery series in print (even if Bernie Gunther is such a close second he is virtually joint first...). The next books in the series are: Dark Fire, Sovereign, Revelation, Heartstone, Lamentation, with Tombland due out later this year. A new Shardlake is an event but, until then, it is wonderful to revisit these books and rediscover how great they are.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6740409998467129013" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1989465811" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1989465811" />
    <a title="Nigel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61463964-nigel"><img alt="Nigel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513313791p2/61463964.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1989465811?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nigel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nigel" href="/user/show/61463964-nigel">Nigel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nigel by: </span>
      Fans of historical fiction and murder mysteries - in the style of Brother Cadfael
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61463964-nigel?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61463964-nigel?shelf=tudor">tudor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61463964-nigel?shelf=monastery">monastery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61463964-nigel?shelf=murder-mystery">murder-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1989465811" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9006565682576517490">This book exceeded my expectations, although on paper it always had a lot going for it in terms of what I usually enjoy - a murder mystery, historical fiction, the first in a series with recurring characters. The time period is during the reign of Henry VIII, familiar to me through other books, most notably Hilary Mantel's 'Wolf Hall' and 'Bring up the Bodies'. Jane Seymour (his second wife) has just died, and Henry VIII has continued his open conflict with the church, now seeking to bring down </span>
  <span id="freeText9006565682576517490" style="display:none">This book exceeded my expectations, although on paper it always had a lot going for it in terms of what I usually enjoy - a murder mystery, historical fiction, the first in a series with recurring characters. The time period is during the reign of Henry VIII, familiar to me through other books, most notably Hilary Mantel's 'Wolf Hall' and 'Bring up the Bodies'. Jane Seymour (his second wife) has just died, and Henry VIII has continued his open conflict with the church, now seeking to bring down the powerful monasteries, which remain a bastion of power and influence of the Catholic Church in England. Thomas Cromwell, the king's 'Mr Fix-it' has been tasked with the 'Dissolution' of the monasteries. Cromwell, who is much less sympathetically portrayed in these books in comparison to Hilary Mantel's depiction, tasks Matthew Shardlake, a hunchbacked lawyer, with investigating the death of a royal commissioner at a monastery. The tale, told in the first person by Shardlake, is essentially a classic murder mystery, but in the setting of a monastery in the Tudor period. Very enjoyable, and vastly superior to 'In the Name of the Rose', with which it shares quite a few similarities. This is the first of a series, and I will definitely be reading more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9006565682576517490" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2868753925" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2868753925" />
    <a title="Richard Benitez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37551400-richard-benitez"><img alt="Richard Benitez" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2868753925?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Benitez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Benitez" href="/user/show/37551400-richard-benitez">Richard Benitez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2868753925" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16457529867257717252">I purposely ordered book precisely because as a historical mystery it covered a period after Thomas More. I was swayed by the author’s credentials as writer of 80 novels and PhD in history. I know nothing of the early period of the Dissolution of the monasteries. What could go wrong? <br/><br/>Everything. The book reads like a religious tract. It goes out its way to denigrate everything “papist” with outrageous propaganda used by the Anglican Church and then successive Protestant. There is a lot of modern</span>
  <span id="freeText16457529867257717252" style="display:none">I purposely ordered book precisely because as a historical mystery it covered a period after Thomas More. I was swayed by the author’s credentials as writer of 80 novels and PhD in history. I know nothing of the early period of the Dissolution of the monasteries. What could go wrong? <br /><br />Everything. The book reads like a religious tract. It goes out its way to denigrate everything “papist” with outrageous propaganda used by the Anglican Church and then successive Protestant. There is a lot of modern scholarship which shows the English and the church prior to Henry viii were the most fervent and good Catholics compared to most of Europe. One of the main problems of the church was they were too good which accounts for its vast wealth. The novel portrays the monks as fat, drunks, ignorant, ugly, smelly, card playing, lustful and with a healthy dose of sodomites. The author plays the audience as fools. The church could not have been so rich and powerful without a strong sense of dedication . The author wallows in the public’s ignorance to sell books. It’s a disgusting cynical ploy on part of the author. I’m not amused. And I’m upset none of the reviewers of these novels mention this historical trash here, on YouTube or Amazon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16457529867257717252" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('QfpBbaSt+7ospI8HX9UL/aD3QxG+x6iIh9nKTcT+CqsTexWlIkqRmUk1BAw17QqVVKy6UmEQk9yRIBAf1yc8iQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Lc1uY3+KbrFMkoO9PaCMv59nawfMnxClYtzS6yjSgoR/TDqr+W0EkikDCLZXmI3XazySRBNIK/F0JQi5Owu0pg==')})
  }

Parris\'s gripping novel, Bruno\'s pursuit of this rare knowledge brings him to London, where he is unexpectedly recruited by Queen Elizabeth I and is sent undercover to Oxford University on the pretext of a royal visitation. As Bruno begins to discover a pattern in these killings, he realizes that no one at Oxford is who he seems to be. Bruno must attempt to outwit a killer who appears obsessed with the boundary between truth and heresy. \n\nLike The Dante Club and The Alienist, this clever, sophisticated, exceptionally enjoyable When Bruno learns that Sophia has been accused of murdering her husband, a prominent magistrate in Canterbury, he agrees to do anything he can to help clear her name.      In the city that was once England\'s greatest center of pilgrimage, Bruno begins to uncover unsuspected secrets that point to the dead man being part of a larger and more dangerous plot in the making. He must turn his detective\'s eye on history—on Saint Thomas Becket, the twelfth-century archbishop murdered in Canterbury Cathedral, and on the legend surrounding the disappearance of his body—in order to solve the crime.      As Bruno\'s feelings for Sophia grow more intense, so does his fear that another murder is about to take place—perhaps his own. But more than Bruno\'s life is at stake in this vividly rendered, impeccably researched, and addictively page-turning whodunit—the stability of the kingdom hangs in the balance as Bruno hunts down a Her brother, passionate abolitionist Tad Archer, had been about to expose a secret that he believed could cause irreparable damage to the British slaving industry. He’d said people were trying to kill him, and now he is missing . . .\n\nTo discover what happened to Tad, Harry is forced to pick up the threads of his friend\'s investigation, delving into the heart of the conspiracy Tad had unearthed. His investigation will threaten his political prospects, his family’s happiness, and force a reckoning with his past, risking the revelation of secrets that have the power to destroy him.\n\nAnd that is only if he can survive the mortal dangers awaiting him in Deptford...<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"11725048784920775526\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14854359474384644275">Christopher John "C.J." Sansom is an English writer of crime novels. He was born in 1952 and was educated at the University of Birmingham, where he took a BA and then a PhD in history. After working in a variety of jobs, he decided to retrain as a solicitor. He practised for a while in Sussex as a lawyer for the disadvantaged, before quitting in order to work full-time as a writer.<br/>He came to promi</span>
  <span id="freeText14854359474384644275" style="display:none">Christopher John "C.J." Sansom is an English writer of crime novels. He was born in 1952 and was educated at the University of Birmingham, where he took a BA and then a PhD in history. After working in a variety of jobs, he decided to retrain as a solicitor. He practised for a while in Sussex as a lawyer for the disadvantaged, before quitting in order to work full-time as a writer.<br />He came to prominence with his series set in the reign of Henry VIII in the 16th century, whose main character is the hunchbacked lawyer Matthew Shardlake. Shardlake works on commission initially from Thomas Cromwell in Dissolution and Dark Fire and then Thomas Cranmer in Sovereign and Revelation.<br /><br />The BBC have commissioned an adaptation of Dissolution with the actor Kenneth Branagh set to star as Shardlake. The rest of the Shardlake books are expected to follow. C. J. Sansom has been consulted on the series, which is in the final stages of negotiation.[citation needed].<br /><br />He has also written Winter in Madrid, a thriller set in Spain in 1940 in the aftermath of the Spanish Civil War.<br /><br />Dark Fire won the 2005 Ellis Peters Historical Dagger, awarded by the Crime Writers' Association (CWA). Sansom himself was "Very Highly Commended" in the 2007 CWA Dagger in the Library award, for the Shardlake series.<br /><br />(from Wikipedia®)</span>
  <a data-text-id="14854359474384644275" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

King Henry VIII is slowly, painfully dying. His Protestant and Catholic councilors are engaged in a final and decisive power struggle; whoever wins will control the government. As heretics are hunted across London, and radical Protestants are burned at the stake, the Catholic party focuses its attack on Henry\'s sixth wife - and Matthew Shardlake\'s old mentor - Queen Catherine Parr.\n\nShardlake, still haunted by his narrow escape from death the year before, steps into action when the beleaguered and desperate Queen summons him to Whitehall Palace to help her recover a dangerous manuscript. The Queen has authored a confessional book, Lamentation of a Sinner, so radically Protestant that if it came to the King\'s attention it could bring both her and her sympathizers crashing down. Although the secret book was kept hidden inside a locked chest in the Queen\'s private chamber, it has inexplicably vanished. Only one page has been recovered - clutched in the hand of a murdered London printer.\n\nShardlake\'s investigations take him on a trail that begins among the backstreet printshops of London, but leads him and his trusty assistant Jack Barak into the dark and labyrinthine world of court politics, a world Shardlake swore never to enter again. In this crucible of power and ambition, Protestant friends can be as dangerous as Catholic enemies, and those with shifting allegiances can be the most dangerous of all.\n\n©2015 C.J.
